<a href="/wiki/Social_anthropology" title="Social anthropology">Social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropology" title="Cultural anthropology">cultural anthropology</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Anthropology_of_religion" title="Template:Anthropology of religion"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Anthropology_of_religion" title="Template talk:Anthropology of religion"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Anthropology_of_religion&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wayang_Kulit_of_Semar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Wayang_Kulit_of_Semar.jpg/220px-Wayang_Kulit_of_Semar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Wayang_Kulit_of_Semar.jpg/330px-Wayang_Kulit_of_Semar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Wayang_Kulit_of_Semar.jpg/440px-Wayang_Kulit_of_Semar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="2105" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wayang_Kulit_of_Semar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Wayang" title="Wayang">Wayang</a> puppet representing <a href="/wiki/Semar" title="Semar">Semar</a></div></div></div>
<p><i><b>Kejawèn</b></i> or <b>Javanism</b>, also called <b>Kebatinan</b>, <i>Agama Jawa</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Aliran_kepercayaan" title="Aliran kepercayaan">Kepercayaan</a></i>, is a <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Javanese</a> religious tradition, consisting of an amalgam of <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> aspects. It is rooted in <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">Javanese</a> history and religiosity, <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretizing</a> aspects of different religions.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term <i>kebatinan</i> is being used interchangeably with <i>kejawèn</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <i>Agama Jawa</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982501_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982501-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Aliran_kepercayaan" title="Aliran kepercayaan">Kepercayaan</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHooker1988196_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHooker1988196-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> although they are not exactly the same:
</p>
<ul><li>Kebatinan: "the science of the inner",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> "inwardness",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> derived from the Arabic word <i>batin</i>, meaning "inner" or "hidden".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevenda201172_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevenda201172-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Kejawèn: "Javanism",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200516_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200516-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> the culture and religious beliefs and practices of the <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">Javanese people</a> of <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central Java</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Java" title="East Java">East Java</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOey200058-59_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOey200058-59-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200516_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200516-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It is "not a religious category, but refers to an ethic and a style of life that is inspired by Javanist thinking".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200517_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200517-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Agama Jawa: "the Javanese religion"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982501_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982501-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aliran_kepercayaan" title="Aliran kepercayaan">Kepercayaan</a>: "belief",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHooker1988196_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHooker1988196-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> "faith",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> full term: <i>Kepercayaan kepada Tuhan Yang Maha Esa</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-UNHCR_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNHCR-9">&#91;web 1&#93;</a></sup> "Believer in One Mighty God".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> "Kepercayaan" is an official cover term for various forms of mysticism in Indonesia. According to Caldarola, it "is not an apt characterization of what the mystical groups have in common".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It includes <i>kebatinan</i>, <i>kejiwan</i> and <i>kerohanian</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982539,_note_30-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><i>Kebatinan</i> is the inner-directed cultivation of inner peace, rooted in pre-Islamic traditions,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevenda201173_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevenda201173-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> whereas <i>kejawèn</i> is outer-directed and community-oriented, manifesting in rituals and practices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevenda201173_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevenda201173-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, <a href="/wiki/Javanese_culture" title="Javanese culture">Javanese culture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Indonesia" title="Religion in Indonesia">Religion in Indonesia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Wajangfiguur_van_karbouwenhuid_de_mythische_vogel_Garuda_voorstellend_TMnr_1772-697.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Wajangfiguur_van_karbouwenhuid_de_mythische_vogel_Garuda_voorstellend_TMnr_1772-697.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Wajangfiguur_van_karbouwenhuid_de_mythische_vogel_Garuda_voorstellend_TMnr_1772-697.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Wajangfiguur_van_karbouwenhuid_de_mythische_vogel_Garuda_voorstellend_TMnr_1772-697.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Wajangfiguur_van_karbouwenhuid_de_mythische_vogel_Garuda_voorstellend_TMnr_1772-697.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Wajangfiguur_van_karbouwenhuid_de_mythische_vogel_Garuda_voorstellend_TMnr_1772-697.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Wajangfiguur_van_karbouwenhuid_de_mythische_vogel_Garuda_voorstellend_TMnr_1772-697.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Wajangfiguur_van_karbouwenhuid_de_mythische_vogel_Garuda_voorstellend_TMnr_1772-697.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Wayang" title="Wayang">Wayang</a> puppet representing <a href="/wiki/Garuda" title="Garuda">Garuda</a></div></div></div>
<p>Java has been a melting pot of religions and cultures, which has created a broad range of religious belief, including animism, spirit cults, and cosmology.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism_and_Buddhism">Hinduism and Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Hinduism and Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Indonesia" title="Hinduism in Indonesia">Hinduism in Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Indonesia" title="Buddhism in Indonesia">Buddhism in Indonesia</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">Indian</a> influences came firstly in the form of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, which reached the <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Indonesia" title="List of islands of Indonesia">Indonesian Archipelago</a> as early as the first century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDaniel2010_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDaniel2010-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> By the fourth century, the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Kutai" title="Kutai">Kutai</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Kalimantan" title="East Kalimantan">East Kalimantan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarumanagara" title="Tarumanagara">Tarumanagara</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Java" title="West Java">West Java</a>, and Holing (<a href="/wiki/Kalingga" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalingga">Kalingga</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central Java</a>, were among the early Hindu states established in the region. Several notable ancient Indonesian Hindu kingdoms are <a href="/wiki/Mataram_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Mataram Kingdom">Mataram</a>, famous for the construction of the majestic <a href="/wiki/Prambanan" title="Prambanan">Prambanan</a> temple, followed by <a href="/wiki/Kediri_(historical_kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kediri (historical kingdom)">Kediri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singhasari" title="Singhasari">Singhasari</a>. Since then Hinduism, along with <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, spread across the archipelago and reached the peak of its influence in the fourteenth century. The last and largest of the Hindu-Buddhist Javanese empires, that of the <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">Majapahit</a>, influenced the entire Indonesian archipelago.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> penetrated deeply into all aspects of society, blending with the indigenous tradition and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> One <a href="/wiki/Conduit_(spiritualism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conduit (spiritualism)">conduit</a> for this were the <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">ascetics</a>, called "resi," (Sanskrit <a href="/wiki/Rishi" title="Rishi">rishi</a>) who taught a variety of mystical practices. A resi lived surrounded by students, who took care of their master's daily needs. Resi's authorities were merely ceremonial. At the courts, <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmin</a> clerics and <i>pudjangga</i> (sacred literati) legitimized rulers and linked <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> cosmology to their political needs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Presently, small <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> enclaves are scattered throughout Java, but there is a large <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> population along the eastern coast nearest <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>, especially around the town of <a href="/wiki/Banyuwangi" class="mw-redirect" title="Banyuwangi">Banyuwangi</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Indonesia" title="Islam in Indonesia">Islam in Indonesia</a></div>
<p>Java adopted<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> Islam around 1500 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Islam was first accepted by the elites and upper echelons of society, which contributed to the further spread and acceptance. Sufism and other versions of <a href="/wiki/Folk_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk Islam">Folk Islam</a> were most easily integrated into the existing folk religion of Java.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The learned versions of Sufi Islam and Shari`a-oriented Islam were integrated at the courts, blending with the rituals and myths of the existing Hindu-Buddhist culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Clifford Geertz described this as <a href="/wiki/Abangan" title="Abangan">abangan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Priyayi" title="Priyayi">priyayi</a>; "the lower class and elite varieties of Javanese syncretism".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kyai" title="Kyai">Kyai</a>, the Muslim scholar of the writ became the new religious elite as Hindu influences receded. Islam recognises no hierarchy of religious leaders nor a formal <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">priesthood</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch colonial government</a> established an elaborate rank order for mosque and other Islamic preaching schools. In Javanese <i><a href="/wiki/Pesantren" title="Pesantren">pesantren</a></i> (Islamic schools), The <i>Kyai</i> perpetuated the tradition of the <i>resi</i>. Students around him provided his needs, even <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasants</a> around the school.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Indonesia" title="Christianity in Indonesia">Christianity in Indonesia</a></div>
<p>Christianity was brought to Java by Portuguese traders and missionaries, from the Dutch Reformed Church, and in the 20th century also by Roman Catholics, such as the <a href="/wiki/Jesuits" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Word_Missionaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Divine Word Missionaries">Divine Word Missionaries</a>. Nowadays there are <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> communities, mostly Reformed in the larger cities, though some rural areas of south-central Java are strongly <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a>. Roman Catholics and other Christian groups have been persecuted for their beliefs such as a ban on Christmas services  or decorations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpa2010_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpa2010-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam_and_kebatinan">Islam and kebatinan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Islam and kebatinan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Nowadays more than 90 percent of the people of Java are Muslims, on a broad continuum between <i><a href="/wiki/Abangan" title="Abangan">abangan</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Santri" title="Santri">santri</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Although Java is nominally Islamitic, <i>kejawen</i>, the syncretic Javanese culture, is a strong undercurrent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder2005_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder2005-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Pre-Islamic Javan traditions have encouraged Islam in a mystical direction.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Some Javanese texts relate stories about <a href="/w/index.php?title=Syekh_Siti_Jenar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Syekh Siti Jenar (page does not exist)">Syekh Siti Jenar</a> (also known as Syekh Lemah Abang) who had conflicts with <a href="/wiki/Wali_Sanga" title="Wali Sanga">Wali Sanga</a>, the nine Islamic scholars in Java, and the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Demak" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Demak">Sultanate of Demak</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeadley2004367-368_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeadley2004367-368-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAzra2006129_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAzra2006129-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Although Syekh Siti Jenar was a <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">sufi</a> whose teaching were similar with <a href="/wiki/Al-Hallaj" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Hallaj">Al-Hallaj</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> most of his followers (<i>Ki Kebo Kenanga</i>) come from Kebatinan.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Some historians have doubted the existence of Syekh Siti Jenar, suggesting the stories represent conflicts between Kebatinan and Islam in the past.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>With the Islamisation of Java there emerged a loosely structured society of religious leadership, revolving around <a href="/wiki/Kyai" title="Kyai">kyais</a>, Islamic experts possessing various degrees of proficiency in pre-Islamic and Islamic <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">lore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> and practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The kyais are the principal intermediaries between the villages masses and the realm of the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>. However, this very looseness of kyai leadership structure has promoted <a href="/wiki/Schism_(religion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Schism (religion)">schism</a>. There were often sharp divisions between orthodox kyais, who merely instructed in Islamic law, with those who taught <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a> and those who sought reformed Islam with modern scientific concepts.
</p><p>As a result, the Javanese recognize two broad streams of religious commitment:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200515_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200515-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><i><a href="/wiki/Santri" title="Santri">Santri</a></i> or <i>putihan</i> ("pure ones"), those who pray, performing the five obligatory daily ritual prayers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200515_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200515-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> They are more orthodox in their Islamic belief and practice,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and oppose the <i>abangan</i>, who they consider to be heterodox.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200515-16_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200515-16-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Abangan" title="Abangan">Abangan</a></i>, "the red ones", who do not strictly observe the Islamic rituals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200515_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200515-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> They have mixed pre-Islamic <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a> and Hindu-Indian concepts with a superficial acceptance of Islamic belief,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and emphasize the importance of the purity of the inner person, the <i>batin</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200515_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200515-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>This distinction between "the High Islam or scripturalist, shari`a-oriented Islam of the `ulama"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and "living local Islam"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> or "<a href="/wiki/Folk_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk Islam">Folk Islam</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> or "popular Islam"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> is not restricted to Java, but can be found in other Islamic countries as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ernest_Gellner" title="Ernest Gellner">Ernest Gellner</a> has developed an influential model of Muslim society, in which this dichotomy is central:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>He sees a dialectical relationship between the two, with periods of scripturalist dominance followed by relapses into emotional, mystical, magical folk Islam. Modernity — especially urbanisation and mass literacy — unsettles the balance between the two, by eroding the social bases of folk Islam. An irreversible shift to scripturalist Islam occurs, which is in Gellner’s view the equivalent of secularisation in the West.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Bruinessen finds this too limited, and distinguishes three overlapping spheres:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Shari`a-oriented Islam,</li>
<li>Sufism (mystical Islam, which has its learned and popular variants),</li>
<li>The periphery of local rituals, local shrines, local spirit cults and heterodox beliefs and practices in general.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Javanese syncretistic religiousness has a strong popular base, outnumbering the santri and the support for Islamic political parties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200522-24_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200522-24-27">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rogge2009_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogge2009-21">&#91;web 2&#93;</a></sup> Choy relates this to a Javanese apparent openness to new religions, but filtering out only those elements which fit into the Javanese culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109-28">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Choy mentions several reasons for this nominal Islamic identity:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109-110_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109-110-29">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The Islamic scholars in Java have been trained in curricula which were geared for social conditions of two or three centuries ago, lacking the ability to impart the spirit and sense of Islam;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109-28">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The inability to summarise the principles of Islam in understandable basic points which can be applied to daily life;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109-110_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109-110-29">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Kebatinan can be learned and understood without the need to learn Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999110_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999110-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>In the early 20th century, several groups became formalised, developing systemetised teachings and rituals, thus offering a 'high' form of <i>abangan</i> religiosity, as an alternative to the 'high' Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMasus2009148_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMasus2009148-31">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Bruinessen opines that the kebatinan-movements is a deliberate rejection of scriptural Islam,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000b_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000b-32">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> which arose out of "<a href="/wiki/Folk_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk Islam">folk Islam</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Java_man_gezeten_onder_een_waringinboom_TMnr_60020257.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Java_man_gezeten_onder_een_waringinboom_TMnr_60020257.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Java_man_gezeten_onder_een_waringinboom_TMnr_60020257.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="306" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Java_man_gezeten_onder_een_waringinboom_TMnr_60020257.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Java_man_gezeten_onder_een_waringinboom_TMnr_60020257.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Java_man_gezeten_onder_een_waringinboom_TMnr_60020257.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Java_man_gezeten_onder_een_waringinboom_TMnr_60020257.jpg 2x" data-file-width="503" data-file-height="700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Java_man_gezeten_onder_een_waringinboom_TMnr_60020257.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Javanese man meditating under a <a href="/wiki/Banyan" title="Banyan">Banyan</a> tree. <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a>, before 1940.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aim">Aim</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Aim">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Batin_(Islam)" title="Batin (Islam)">Batin (Islam)</a></div>
<p><i>Kebatinan</i> is derived from the Arabic word <i>batin</i>, meaning "inner" or "hidden",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevenda201172_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevenda201172-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> or "inner self".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999107_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999107-33">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> It is a metaphysical search for harmony within one's inner self, connection with the universe, and with an Almighty God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999107_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999107-33">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <i>Kebatinan</i> believe in a "super-consciousness" which can be contacted through meditation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999110_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999110-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Kebatinan</i> is a combination of metaphysics, mysticism and other esoteric doctrines<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999107_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999107-33">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> from Animistic, Hinduistic, Buddhist and Islamic origins.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Although the Javanese culture is tolerant, and open to new religions, only those qualities are accepted and filtered which fit into the Javanese culture, character and personality.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999109-28">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Javanese ideals combine human wisdom (<i>wicaksana</i>), psyche (<i>waskita</i>) and perfection (<i>sempurna</i>). The follower must control his/her passions, eschewing earthly riches and comforts, so that he/she may one day reach enlightened harmony and union with the spirit of the universe.
</p><p>According to Choy, the <i>Kebatinan</i> have no certain prophet,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999108_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999108-34">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> sacred book,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999108_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999108-34">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> nor distinct religious festivals and rituals. Nevertheless, various kebatinan-movements have their own foundational writings and founders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999-35">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMasud2009148_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMasud2009148-36">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A <i>kebatinan</i> practitioner can identify with one of the six officially recognized religions, while still subscribe to the <i>kebatinan</i> belief and way of life.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership">Membership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Membership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although <i>kebatinan</i> is a predominantly Javanese tradition, it has also attracted practitioners from other ethnic and religious groups, such as Chinese and Buddhists,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999111-112_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999111-112-37">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and foreigners from Australia and Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Suharto" title="Suharto">Suharto</a> counted himself as one of its adherents.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Their total membership is difficult to estimate as many of their adherents identify themselves with one of the official religions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeatty1999_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeatty1999-38">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_recognition">Official recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Official recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pancasila_(politics)" title="Pancasila (politics)">Indonesian state ideology</a> strives toward a unified nation, recognizing only monotheism. Meanwhile, there is also a tolerance for non-recognized religions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder2005_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder2005-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> A broad plurality of religions and sects exist. In the middle of 1956, the Department of Religious Affairs in <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a> reported 63 religious sects in Java other than the official Indonesian religions. Of these, 22 were in <a href="/wiki/West_Java" title="West Java">West Java</a>, 35 were in <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central Java</a>,  and 6 in <a href="/wiki/East_Java" title="East Java">East Java</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Kroef1961-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These include also kebatinan-groups, such as Sumarah.  This loosely organized current of thought and practice was legitimized in the 1945 constitution,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> but failed to attain official recognition as a religion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 1973 it was recognized as <i>Kepercayaan kepada Tuhan Yang Maha Esa</i> (Indonesian: Belief in One Mighty God<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>), but withdrawn from the jurisdiction of the Ministry of Religion and placed under the jurisdiction of the Ministry of Education and Culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999112-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices">Practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tapas_(Indian_religions)" title="Tapas (Indian religions)">Tapas (Indian religions)</a></div>
<p>A variety of practices is being used in kebatinan to acquire <i>ilmu</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERetsikas2012179_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERetsikas2012179-39">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup>, namely <i>tiraka</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristomy2008171_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristomy2008171-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERetsikas2012179_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERetsikas2012179-39">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes-Freeland2008189_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes-Freeland2008189-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;note 5&#93;</a></sup> and <i>tapa</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristomy2008171_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristomy2008171-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> or <i>tapabrata</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes-Freeland2008189_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes-Freeland2008189-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;note 6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Kebatinan followers practice in their own way to seek spiritual and emotional relief. These practices are not performed in churches or mosques, but at home or in caves or on mountain perches. Meditation in Javanese culture is a search for inner self wisdom and to gain physical strength. This tradition is passed down from generation to generation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meditation">Meditation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Meditation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are several <i>tapa</i>:
</p>
<ul><li>tapa Ngalong (meditation by hanging from a tree)</li>
<li>tapa Kungkum (meditation under a small waterfall or meeting point of 2-3 rivers / <i>Tempuran</i> / <i>Tjampuhan</i>)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fasting">Fasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fasting is a common practice employed by Javanese spiritualists in order to attain discipline of mind and body to get rid of material and emotional desires:
</p>
<ul><li>pasa Mutih (abstention from eating anything that is salted and sweetened, only eating/drinking pure water &amp; rice)</li>
<li>pasa Senen-Kemis (fasting on Monday-Thursday)</li>
<li>pasa Ngebleng (fasting for a longer period, usually 3-5-7 days)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animistic_worship">Animistic worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Animistic worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Kebatinan</i> often implies animistic worship, because it encourages sacrifices and devotions to local and ancestral spirits. These spirits are believed to inhabit natural objects, human beings, artifacts, and grave sites of important wali (Muslim saints). Illness and other misfortunes are traced to such spirits, and if sacrifices or pilgrimages fail to placate angry deities, the advice of a <i>dukun</i> or healer is sought.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_practices">Other practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other practices include:
</p>
<ul><li>tapa Pati-Geni (avoiding fire or light for a day or days and isolating oneself in dark rooms),</li>
<li>tapa Ngadam  (stand/walk on foot from sunset till sunset, 24 hours in Silence)</li>
<li>the rituals carried out on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kemukus" title="Mount Kemukus">Mount Kemukus</a> (also known as Sex Mountain), which have also been linked to Kejawen.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_texts">Historical texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Historical texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Kebatinan and <i>kejawen</i> practices are extensively written about in texts that are held in the <a href="/wiki/Sonobudoyo_library" class="mw-redirect" title="Sonobudoyo library">Sonobudoyo library</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a>, and the main <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kraton_Libraries&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kraton Libraries (page does not exist)">Kraton Libraries</a> of <a href="/wiki/Surakarta" title="Surakarta">Surakarta</a> and Yogyakarta.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Many of the texts are deliberately elliptical so that those who do not work with either initiates or teachers are unable to ascertain or understand the esoteric doctrines and practices.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In quite a few cases codified texts with secret systems to "unlock" the meanings are employed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>But according to Bruinessen, the writing down of kebatinan teachings was a novelty which appeared with the institutionalisation of the kebatinan-movements in the beginning of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000b_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000b-32">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kebatinan_organisations">Kebatinan organisations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Kebatinan organisations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The appearance of formal kebatinan movements reflects the modernisation of Indonesia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Kebatinan movements appeared early in the 1900s in urban traditional elite circles,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> together with the rise of nationalism and the <a href="/wiki/Muhammadiyah" title="Muhammadiyah">Muhammadiyah</a>, a modernist Islamic movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Hardopusoro, one of the earliest kebatinan-movements, had strong links with the <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Some remained very elitist, while others also accepted lower urban and rural followings, thereby popularising abangan, or syncretistic Islam, as an alternative to shari`a-oriented Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Indonesia gained independence in 1949, the kebatinan received political support and attracted large followings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMusad2009148_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMusad2009148-46">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Kebatinan-movements were seen by secular nationalistic elites as allies against the rise of <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">political Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000b_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000b-32">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The political struggle between the Muslim parties and the Communists and Nationalists lead to a sharper demarcation between syncretistic and shari`a-oriented Islam, whereby most kebatinan movements affiliated with the Communist or Nationalist Parties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;note 7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Umbrella organisations representing several hundred kebatinan organisations, lobbied to attain legitimacy and recognition as an official religion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHooker1988196_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHooker1988196-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> They are registered at the HKP (Himpunan Penghayat Kepercayaan), which is controlled by the PAKEM (Pengawas Aliran Kepercayaan Masyarakat).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> After the Suharto-era (1967-1998), the kebatinan-movements lost political support,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMusad2009148_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMusad2009148-46">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and have become less dynamic, their adherents avoiding public engagement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOoi2004719-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Altogether several hundred kebatinan-groups are or have been registered, the best-known of which are:<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;note 8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Subud<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007226_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007226-59">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116-60">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Sumarah<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007226_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007226-59">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116-60">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Pangestu<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007226_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007226-59">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116-60">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Sapta Dharma<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007226_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007226-59">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116-60">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Majapahit Pancasila<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116_60-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116-60">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subud">Subud</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Subud">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Subud" title="Subud">Subud</a></div>
<p>Subud was founded in the 1920s by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Subuh_Sumohadiwidjojo" title="Muhammad Subuh Sumohadiwidjojo">Muhammad Subuh Sumohadiwidjojo</a>. The name Subud was first used in the late 1940s when Subud was legally registered in Indonesia. The basis of Subud is a spiritual exercise commonly referred to as the <i><a href="/wiki/Latihan" title="Latihan">latihan kejiwaan</a></i>, which was said by Muhammad Subuh to be guidance from "the Power of God" or "the Great Life Force". The aim of Subud is to attain perfection of character according to the will of God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999118_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999118-61">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Only when passion, heart and mind are separated from the inner feeling is it possible to make contact with the "Great Life Force" which permeates everywhere.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999119_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999119-62">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The name <i>Subud</i> is formed from the words <i>susila</i> ("the good character of man"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999118_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999118-61">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>), <i>budhi</i> ("the force of the inner self"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999118_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999118-61">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>) and <i>dharma</i> ("trust in God"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999118_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999118-61">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>). These words are derived from the Sanskrit words <i>susila</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bodhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Bodhi">bodhi</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-SubudBritain_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SubudBritain-63">&#91;web 3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Muhammad Subuh saw the present age as one that demands personal evidence and proof of religious or spiritual realities, as people no longer just believe in words. He claimed that Subud is not a new teaching or religion but only that the latihan kejiwaan itself is the kind of proof that humanity is looking for. He also rejected the classification of Subud as a kebatinan organization. There are now Subud groups in about 83 countries, with a worldwide membership of about 10,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHunt2003122_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHunt2003122-64">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sumarah">Sumarah</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sumarah">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sumarah was formed in the 1930s by Pak Hardo, Pak Sadina and Pak Sutadi, without a formal organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999115_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999115-65">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In those early days, the younger members were taught <i>kanoman</i>, occult practices including invulnerability for knives and guns. This was regarded as essential in the struggle against the Dutch colonial powers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999115_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999115-65">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Around 1950, when Indonesia became an independent nation, Sumarah was streamlined and organised by Dr. Surono. The emphasis shifted from magic to "surrender to God".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999115_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999115-65">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> From 1957 on internal struggles surfaced between dr. Surono and the founders Pak Hardo and Pak Sadina, leading to a change in leadership by dr. Ary Muthy in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999115_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999115-65">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sumarah theology maintains that humankind's soul is like the holy spirit, a spark from the Divine Essence, which means that we are in essence similar to God. In other words, "One can find God within oneself," a belief similar to the "<a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">I</a>=<a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">God</a>" theory found in Hindu-Javanese literature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999114_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999114-66">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Sumarah theology, man and his physical and spiritual world are divided into three parts:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999114_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999114-66">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The physical body and brain. One section, <i>Sukusma</i>, governs the passions. In the brain, the faculty of thinking has two functions:
<ul><li>To record memories</li>
<li>To serve as a means of communion with God</li></ul></li>
<li>The invisible world, which is situated within the chest. It is the <a href="/wiki/Jiva" title="Jiva">Jiwa</a>, the ineffable soul, which provides the driving forces governing thought and reason. It is here that the deeper feeling (<i>Rasa</i>) is located.</li>
<li>The more elusive and sublime world. The most elusive and sublime world is hidden somewhere near the anatomical heart.</li></ul>
<p>Sumarah's conception of God is different from Islam. It has a <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheistic</a> vision of reality, considering God to be present in all living beings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999114_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999114-66">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pangestu">Pangestu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Pangestu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pangestu was founded in 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117-67">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Its doctrine was revealed in 1932 to Sunarto Mertowarjoyo, and written down in the <i>Setat Sasangka Djati</i> by R.T. Harjoparakowo and R. Trihardono Sumodiharjo Pangastu.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117-67">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> It describes the way to obtain <i>wahyu</i>, the blessing of God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117-67">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sapta_Dharma">Sapta Dharma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sapta Dharma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sapta Dharma was founded in 1952 by Harjo Sapura, after he received a revelation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116_60-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116-60">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> According to Sri Pawenang, it was God's wish to provide the Indonesian people with a new spirituality in atime of crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116_60-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116-60">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Its aim is to free man of his passions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117-67">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Sapta Dharma teachings, <i>suji</i> (meditation) is necessary to pierce through different layers of obstacles to reach <a href="/wiki/Semar" title="Semar">Semar</a>, the guardian spirit of Java.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116-117_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999116-117-68">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Theory and practice resemble Hindu <a href="/wiki/Kundalini_yoga" title="Kundalini yoga">Kundalini yoga</a>, aiming at awakening the Kundalini energy and guiding it through the <a href="/wiki/Chakra" title="Chakra">chakras</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117_67-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999117-67">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Majapahit_Pancasila">Majapahit Pancasila</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Majapahit Pancasila">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">Majapahit</a> Pancasila<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;note 9&#93;</a></sup> was founded by W. Hardjanta Pardjapangarsa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999119_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999119-62">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> It is based in Javanese Hindu-yogic practices,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETarling1992563_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETarling1992563-71">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> c.q. <a href="/wiki/Kundalini_yoga" title="Kundalini yoga">Kundalini yoga</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999119_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999119-62">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> rather than Balinese ritual practice as is prevalent in <a href="/wiki/Parisada_Hindu_Dharma" class="mw-redirect" title="Parisada Hindu Dharma">Parisada Hindu Dharma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETarling1992563_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETarling1992563-71">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> According to Hardjanta, his meditation practices also lead to invulnerability for knives, daggers and other weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoy1999122_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoy1999122-72">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_of_kebatinan">Spread of kebatinan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Spread of kebatinan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Malaysia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Kebatinan beliefs have spread to some parts of Malaysia, wherein certain individuals have combined it with Islamic concepts (e.g. proclaiming themselves to be new-age Islamic prophets, but delivering messages that are a combination of Islamic and kebatinan beliefs). This has led to the Malaysian Islamic authorities declaring elements of kebatinan to be "syirik" (<a href="/wiki/Shirk_(Islam)" title="Shirk (Islam)">shirk</a>) and un-Islamic. Kebatinan interpretations of Islam are widespread in Malaysia among practitioners of <a href="/wiki/Silat" title="Silat">silat</a>, traditional healers, and some preachers (such as <a href="/wiki/Ariffin_Mohammed" title="Ariffin Mohammed">Ariffin Mohammed</a> and other self-proclaimed Islamic prophets).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the Netherlands, the former colonial power in Indonesia, some kebatinan-groups are active.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenard2010_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenard2010-73">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the majority of Singaporean Malays are of Indonesian descent, particularly from Java, many of Kebatinan are still practiced usually among older people. However, the practice is still widespread among some Javanese Silat and <a href="/wiki/Kuda_Kepang" class="mw-redirect" title="Kuda Kepang">Kuda Kepang</a> groups, and also traditional shamans.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suriname">Suriname</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Suriname">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Javanism_in_Suriname" class="mw-redirect" title="Javanism in Suriname">Javanism in Suriname</a></div>
<p>It was brought to <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a> by <a href="/wiki/Javanese_Surinamese" title="Javanese Surinamese">Javanese</a> workers in the late 19th century.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kejaw%C3%A8n&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bruinessen: "Java was converted to Islam quite late; the process started seriously around 1500CE, that is, at the time of the great Alevi rebellions. Adoption of Islam is perhaps a better term than conversion, for the Javanese were deliberately syncretistic. For many of the new Muslims Islam, especially in its Sufi variety, was a welcome additional source of spiritual power, not a substitute for what they already had."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Anthropologist Clifford Geertz made a well-known, though criticised, threefold distinction between <i>abangan</i>, <i>antri</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Priyayi" title="Priyayi">priyayi</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rogge2009_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogge2009-21">&#91;web 2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200521-22_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200521-22-22">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The <i>priyayi</i> are the descendants of the high class and court members, were gurus taught the Hindu-Buddhist art of inner cultivation,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200521_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200521-23">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> which stayed alive in the interior areas of Java.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200516_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200516-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Geertz noticed that the <i>priyayi</i> play a central role in the teaching of <i>kejawen</i> and <i>kebatinan</i> to the <i>abangan</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulder200521_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulder200521-23">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bruinessen: "This third sphere was no doubt in most parts of the world for many years the one that had by far the greatest numbers of adherents. It has often been through Sufism that people from the heterodox periphery gradually moved towards some degree of conformity with orthodoxy."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a_14-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Bruinessen2000a-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">knowledge, power</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Fasting",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristomy2008171_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristomy2008171-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> "ascetic exercises",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERetsikas2012179_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERetsikas2012179-39">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> "spiritual techniques"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes-Freeland2008189_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes-Freeland2008189-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">"austerity"</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristomy2008171_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristomy2008171-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> "spiritual techniques"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes-Freeland2008189_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes-Freeland2008189-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The relation between religion c.q. "spirituality", politics and (post-)colonial struggles is not unique to Indonesia. In India, <a href="/wiki/Hindu_reform_movements" title="Hindu reform movements">Hindu reform movements</a> involved both religious and social reforms, for example the <a href="/wiki/Brahmo_Samaj" title="Brahmo Samaj">Brahmo Samaj</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESenari2000_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESenari2000-47">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vivekananda" class="mw-redirect" title="Vivekananda">Vivekananda</a>, who modernised <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERambachan1994_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERambachan1994-48">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aurobindo" class="mw-redirect" title="Aurobindo">Aurobindo</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESenari2000_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESenari2000-47">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESenari2000_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESenari2000-47">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In Buddhist countries, <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_modernism" title="Buddhist modernism">Buddhist modernism</a> was a response against the colonial powers and the western culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008-49">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In Sri Lanka, <a href="/wiki/Theravada_Buddhism" class="mw-redirect" title="Theravada Buddhism">Theravada Buddhism</a> was revitalised in the struggle against the colonial rule. The <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a> played an essential role here.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan200898_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan200898-50">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1996185-188_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1996185-188-51">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFields199283-118_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFields199283-118-52">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In China, <a href="/wiki/Taixu" title="Taixu">Taixu</a> propagated a <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_Buddhism" title="Humanistic Buddhism">Humanistic Buddhism</a>, which is again endorsed by Jing Hui, the (former) abbott of <a href="/wiki/Bailin_Monastery" class="mw-redirect" title="Bailin Monastery">Bailin Monastery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2010189_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2010189-53">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In Japan, Buddhism adopted nationalistic politics to survive in the modern era, in which it lost support from the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVictoria2006_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVictoria2006-54">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008-49">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> zen was popularised in the west by adherents of this modern Buddhism, especially <a href="/wiki/D.T._Suzuki" class="mw-redirect" title="D.T. Suzuki">D.T. Suzuki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hakuun_Yasutani" title="Hakuun Yasutani">Hakuun Yasutani</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFields1992_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFields1992-55">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMahan2008-49">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007225-226_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_BruinessenHowell2007225-226-57">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> for a longer list of Kebatinan organisations.</span>
